Book Reviews

Contemporary Forum (Continued) · Xu · Sleep · Lin 187

By Goldsworthy Lowes Dickinson, Translated by Liang Yuchun

The first point is to critique Canterbury's ideology, which cannot be rigidly fixed. He cites ancient doctrines, "…society is like a pipe organ, but it can only play one simple harmony. The legislator's duty is to tune the organ so that it can accurately play that tone. Therefore, if Plato could follow his ideas, his large-scale universal harmony (the great harmony of producers, soldiers, and philosophers), the place where all members of society gather, would monotonously and rigidly play until now. The harmony Plato envisioned is naturally wonderful, but the discordant sounds he suppressed have been increasing in volume day by day, echoing noisily in the vaults of time from his time to the present. I often think that these discordant sounds contain a greater harmony, more dignified than any harmony he imagined. Moreover, that noise is cleverly woven into a cosmic symphony, too profound for us to understand more than a small part. Only the immaculate wisdom of the gods can appreciate it." He says, "The fundamental flaw of all ideal polities is that no matter how perfect that polity is, it can only be applied to the situation at that time... But that situation should only be a temporary and short process in human history, not to continue so immutably... As for simply rigidifying the current polity, treating it as a precious treasure without adding any ideal elements, the abuses are even more self-evident."

He believes that the grand system of the universe, encompassing everything, self-

Follows natural evolution and change, which is an unavoidable law of nature. If a fixed restriction is imposed, it hinders natural evolution. Therefore, the political ideal of liberalism is a laissez-faire policy that disregards everything. In the end, he says, "We are letting the people do as they please... I believe in people... If I can't trust them, what am I supposed to believe in?"

The second point is to explain the principles of free trade. He believes that this is also a natural fact within the universe. He says, "I dare say that among all human activities, the most obvious expression of God's will is this exchange of products... I want to say that the current situation resulting from the implementation of free policy can prove the benefits of free policy... I believe that soon other countries will also awaken, see their own interests clearly, and establish new laws regarding the economy to compete with us. As a result, everyone will benefit. I see that countries around the world are about to put down political hatred, unite, and engage only in peaceful commercial competition. The booming of cannons has stopped, and only the soft creaking of spinning machines is heard... Humanity will no longer meet on the battlefield, but will meet through their chosen representatives, as our greatest poet said, in 'the parliament of man, the federation of the world.'"

His language is very pure and clear, and his reasoning and judgment are harmonious and tight-knit, absolutely making people feel impeccable.

The third speaker is Mendoza, the current leader of the Conservative Party and Lyndemere's major political rival. His argument is extraordinarily cold, sharp, and witty. He opposes Lyndemere's liberalism. He believes that freedom can never be unlimited. To maintain social balance, there must be many intervening forces, and he uses the police as an example.

His opening statement immediately embarrassed Lyndemere. He said, "A person who does not have the privilege of directly hearing God's instructions..." In one sentence, he was able to reflect that what Lyndemere said had become a non-factual joke. The example he gives is:

"Take the police system as an example... With the help of the police, the weak can manage the strong, and the minority can manage the majority. With his help, many people who should be eliminated in the struggle for survival are actually living... The marriage system is tightly tied to his hem, and the private property system runs into his arms and quietly lies down."

"…If freedom is not guaranteed by any authority, then what is the difference between freedom and chaos, if only in terms of freedom itself? If we do not use the presence or absence of a considerable component of interference to distinguish between freedom and chaos, what other distinction do we have? If we acknowledge this distinction of the presence or absence of an interference component, aren't we going to walk down from the mountain peak of unrestrained prophecy to the barren and itchy plains of political compromise?"

Next, he explains the Conservative Party's political views. He says, "What we pay attention to is not all of humanity, but the British people; not an ideal polity, but the British constitution; not some political economy, but the actual state of our commerce. In this great forest of facts, filled with new and old weeds, secular lazy trees, fat bushes, and beautiful climbing vines, we always follow the old paths, stepping slowly and cautiously, striving to prevent those old paths from being blocked, and daring not to open up new paths. Unless we truly understand that the new path will lead to the destination, we will never cut down the good trees to make a new path."

Furthermore, he describes his views on foreign trade and world alliances, which stand in opposition to Lyndemere and have his own unique insights. He says, "He sees in his imagination a period of peace brought about by commerce, where everyone has good intentions towards each other. He believes that through God's mysterious means, greed and competition will help us achieve benevolence and peace. But I cannot boldly fantasize along with him on this point... Or look forward with hope to that Crystal Palace-style era of great happiness... I believe that the future will contain many wars and rumors of wars. Our Britain, because of its wealth and unprecedented success, will especially be the target of envy and resentment from the people of all Europe. They look around, wanting to find a place to transplant the ever-increasing number of people, only to see that the land is already occupied by the British everywhere, and the British flag is always flying. This is Britain's greatest danger, but I grasp from this point the only hope for our country's future. The territory of Britain is not just those three islands... I believe that our colonies will not fall away from us like ripe fruits, and our dependencies will not fall into the hands of other masters. Sooner or later, Britain will awaken and understand the mission of its great empire. The British people across the sea will be of one heart and one mind with me. Therefore, the great alliance that I foresee is not an alliance of humanity, but a great alliance of the British people in the world."

His thoughts are very representative of the imperialist ideology of the average Englishman.

The fourth speaker is Allison, a moderate socialist and a powerful critic of both parties.

He first briefly criticizes the opinions of Lyndemere and Mendoza, and then explains the socialist theory he advocates. He believes that "nature" is a chaotic state without rules, and all culture contains the meaning of restraint. Socialism is of course also restraint. Socialism is revolutionary, and liberalism, if it is not empty talk, should also be revolutionary. Revolution does not contain the act of rebellion. The act of rebellion is only the miscarriage of the revolution. The meaning of socialism is to increase the effectiveness of social organization. Political power should be expanded. The reason why the people do not trust the government now is that the current government is an organ for the benefit of special classes. Socialism is to replace the government of corrupt officials with a clean government of the common people. Social organization should pay attention to the cultivation of new ethics, new habits, and new religions. The only fundamental creed of socialism is that human progress depends entirely on "conquest". Nature, and science are people's only weapon to "conquer" nature.

Finally, he describes his attitude of faith. He says, "We can be said to be cooperating with the force of the universe... Because the evolution of things will naturally reach the point we hope for... Even if we stop rowing, our boat will still move forward. If we are temporarily blocked, the currents that hinder our boat's progress only belong to one or two special places, not the trend of the entire current. Among all politicians, only we have faith, but our faith is based on science, so it is a permanent and unshakable faith."

With his arrival, the direction of the conversation changed from political views to a discussion of socialism.

(To be continued)
